        </a>
       </li>

       &lt;!--

   <div id="innerContent">
    <div id="innerLeft">
     <div class="post">
      <div class="featuredTitleOuterContainer">
       <div class="featuredTitleImageContainer">
        <a href="https://falkvinge.net/2014/02/10/placing-your-crypto-wealth-in-cold-storage-installing-armory-on-ubuntu/" rel="bookmark" title="Placing Your Cryptocoin Wealth In Cold Storage: Installing Armory On Ubuntu">
         <img alt="ThreeInterlockedCogs" class="FeaturedImage wp-post-image" height="363" src="//falkvinge.net/files/2011/05/ThreeInterlockedCogs-646x363.jpg" width="646"/>
        </a>
       </div>
       <div class="featuredTitleTextContainer">
        <h1 class="featuredTitle">
         Placing Your Cryptocoin Wealth In Cold Storage: Installing Armory On Ubuntu
        </h1>
       </div>
       <div class="authorAvatarContainer" style="position:absolute;bottom:88px;right:10px;height:58px;width:58px;pointer-events:none;border: 1px solid #222">
        <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-58 photo" height="58" src="//1.gravatar.com/avatar/de4236cce80d1c250bd9e98b612db624?s=58&amp;d=mm&amp;r=r" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/de4236cce80d1c250bd9e98b612db624?s=116&amp;d=mm&amp;r=r 2x" width="58"/>
       </div>
       <div style="position:absolute;bottom:5px;right:10px;width:60px;height:40px;pointer-events:none">
        <img src="//falkvinge.net/wp-content/themes/WpNewspaper/images/callout-60px.png" style="background:none;border:none;padding:0px;border:none"/>
       </div>
       <div style="position:absolute;bottom:21px;right:10px;width:60px;height:20px;font-weight:400;font-size:18px;text-align:center;color:white;pointer-events:none;line-height:17px">
        34
       </div>
       <div class="plusOneCount" rel="https://falkvinge.net/2014/02/10/placing-your-crypto-wealth-in-cold-storage-installing-armory-on-ubuntu/" style="position:absolute;bottom:48px;right:12px;height:20px;width:60px;font-style:italic;font-weight:500;text-align:center;pointer-events:none;font-size:20px;line-height:17px;color:white;text-shadow:#444 1px 1px 1px, black 1px 1px">
       </div>
       <div style="position:absolute;bottom:15px;left:15px;background-color:black;border:solid 1px white;pointer-events:none;opacity:.5">
        <p style="color:white;padding-left:8px;padding-right:10px;padding-top:4px;padding-bottom:6px;font-weight:500;line-height:18px;font-size:20px;line-height:20px;text-transform:uppercase">
         Cryptocurrency  Rick Falkvinge
        </p>
       </div>
       <div style="position:absolute;bottom:15px;left:15px;background-color:transparent;pointer-events:none">
        <p style="color:white;padding-left:9px;padding-right:11px;padding-top:5px;padding-bottom:7px;font-weight:500;line-height:18px;font-size:20px;line-height:20px;text-transform:uppercase">
         Cryptocurrency  Rick Falkvinge
        </p>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p class="intro">
       As bitcoin continues to climb in value, its security flaws become increasingly apparent and important. The nightmare is waking up one morning to discover you were hacked and your five-, six-, or seven-figure savings are gone forever. Heres how to install
       <em>
        cold storage
       </em>
       to eliminate the risk of such a hack.
      </p>
      <p>
       Youll find no shortage of people on the Bitcoin forums saying that your private keys shouldnt be on any computer connected to the Internet. This reveals one of the major usability flaws with bitcoin today  the impossibility of trusting your wealth to stay, well,
       <em>
        your
       </em>
       wealth, and how that is a major roadblock to mainstream adoption. But while the community works on that problem, the early adopters among us have the ability to make sure the keys to our bitcoin wealth cant be hacked. Thats a far from trivial exercise, so heres a walkthrough on how to do that.
      </p>
      <p>
       <strong>
        You will need:
       </strong>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Your ordinary workstation, connected to the Internet
       </li>
       <li>
        An old laptop, which has had wi-fi permanently disabled in hardware
       </li>
       <li>
        A fresh-from-package USB stick (1G is enough)
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The object of this exercise is to make a laptop that has never connected, nor will ever connect to the Internet  and have that laptop be the so-called
       <em>
        cold storage
       </em>
       of the keys that are used to send coins from our large wallets. We will still be able to see the wallet from our online workstation, but in order to send any money from it, we will need to do so on the offline laptop, the cold storage. To minimize risk of infection, well be installing Ubuntu Linux on the laptop (nothing from Microsoft, nothing from Apple).
      </p>
      <p>
       <strong>
        Steps to perform:
       </strong>
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        Download Ubuntu LTS and create a boot stick
       </li>
       <li>
        Install Ubuntu LTS on the laptop
       </li>
       <li>
        Install apt-offline, update, upgrade
       </li>
       <li>
        Install dependencies then Armory
       </li>
       <li>
        Create your offline wallet
       </li>
       <li>
        Make a fragmented secure paper backup (m-of-n)
       </li>
       <li>
        Make and install your watch-only copy on your workstation
       </li>
       <li>
        Wipe and reinstall laptop to make sure the backup works
       </li>
       <li>
        Send your first few millicoins to test the wallet
       </li>
       <li>
        Distribute your backup fragments
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       This is easily a workdays exercise, but this walkthrough is going to save you from a lot of the trial-and-error and googling that took most of my time.
      </p>
      <h3>
       Get an offline laptop and a USB stick
      </h3>
      <p>
       Your first step is to take an old laptop and kill its wi-fi. We want to make sure that this particular computer can never, ever, again connect to the Internet  even accidentally,
       <em>
        particularly
       </em>
       not accidentally. It has to be permanently offline. We are going to move files back and forth to it using a USB stick.
      </p>
      <p>
       The laptop doesnt need to have particularly strong specs  512M of RAM is plenty. Mine is almost a decade old. We assume here that you trust the laptops hardware for reliability and for not having been infected hardware-wise. Obviously, you shouldnt pick a laptop that has had any chance of being tampered with by an adversary.
      </p>
      <p>
       Get a USB stick. To minimize risk of infection, use a completely fresh one: break its packaging.
      </p>
      <h3>
       Get Ubuntu LTS
      </h3>
      <p>
       Download Ubuntu 12.04.4 LTS to your workstation
       <a href="http://releases.ubuntu.com/precise/ubuntu-12.04.4-alternate-i386.iso">
        from here
       </a>
       . 32-bit is fine. Make care to use the
       <em>
        alternate installer
       </em>
       , which the link points to, as were going to use full-disk encryption on the offline laptop, so that an adversary cant obtain your wealth even if they obtain the laptop.
      </p>
      <p>
       Open the package of a new USB stick and run Startup Disk Creator, installing the ISO to the USB stick and making it bootable. (If youre not running Ubuntu Linux on your workstation, use whatever utility available to you to create a bootable USB stick from an ISO.)
      </p>
      <h3>
       Install Ubuntu Linux on the laptop
      </h3>
      <p>
       Did you kill wi-fi on the laptop yet? No you didnt. Seriously, the one single purpose of this laptop is to never ever have been connected to the Internet, so take the time for peace of mind and kill that wi-fi. On laptops this age, theres usually a physical switch. You could even glue it shut in the wifi off position if you dont want to open the laptop and resolder the switch to permanently off.
      </p>
      <p>
       With that done, install Ubuntu 12.04.4 LTS from the alternate installer. It may seem unfamiliar as its slightly more technical than the standard installer, but the steps are very straightforward.
      </p>
      <p>
       The one key step you
       <em>
        must
       </em>
       make sure to do here is to choose full-disk encryption. You do that in the partitioning part of the install sequence: make sure to pick the installation option named
       <em>
        <strong>
         Guided  Use entire disk and set up
         <u>
          encrypted
         </u>
         LVM
        </strong>
       </em>
       . That option is the one reason were using the alternate installer in the first place. Choose a
       <a href="http://xkcd.com/936/">
        strong password
       </a>
       , obviously. Battery horse staple correct. Use five or six words if you want to be super-paranoid.
      </p>
      <p>
       Install and login.
      </p>
      <h3>
       Install apt-offline, update, upgrade
      </h3>
      <p>
       <em>
        <u>
         Technical note:
        </u>
        This section assumes that youre running a version of Debian Linux, such as Ubuntu, on your online workstation. If youre not, the utility apt-offline is available for your workstation even if youre running something else, like Microsoft Windows. Youll have to find and install it yourself, though, and find the appropriate syntax, adapting whats specified below to your operating system.
       </em>
      </p>
      <p>
       Ok, so you have a freshly-installed offline laptop with 32-bit Ubuntu 12.04.4 LTS on it. The laptop cant update its software. Thats part of the point, actually, but in this case, it causes a few problems as we need to install the software packages required for Armory.
      </p>
      <p>
       The solution is named
       <em>
        apt-offline
       </em>
       , which is a utility that enables an offline computer to use a secondary, online computer to fetch its packages from the Internet. From your online workstation, get
       <a href="http://se.archive.ubuntu.com/ubuntu/pool/universe/a/apt-offline/apt-offline_1.1.1build1_all.deb">
        this debian package
       </a>
       and put it on the USB stick (dont erase the boot packages also on there just yet). Move the USB stick to the laptop, open a Terminal, and run
      </p>
      <pre>
       <code>
        sudo dpkg -i /media/[your USB stick]/apt-offline_1.1.1build1_all.deb
       </code>
      </pre>
      <p>
       This will enable us to install packages using the USB stick as a relay to the Internet, going through the online workstation.
      </p>
      <p>
       First, an update/upgrade sequence. On the
       <strong>
        offline
       </strong>
       laptop, with the USB stick still inserted,
      </p>
      <pre>
       <code>
        sudo apt-offline set --update /media/[your USB stick]/cold-apt.sig
       </code>
      </pre>
      <p>
       Move the USB stick to the
       <strong>
        online
       </strong>
       workstation, then run
      </p>
      <pre>
       <code>
        sudo apt-get install apt-offline
       </code>
      </pre>
      <p>
       (Yes, we need apt-offline on the online workstation as well! They can be different distributions and architectures, thats ok. If youre not running a Debian Linux or an Ubuntu Linux, find the way of installing and running apt-offline that is appropriate for whatever your online workstation runs on.)
      </p>
      <pre>
       <code>
        mkdir /media/[your USB stick]/apt-offline
       </code>
      </pre>
      <p>
       Well be storing the downloaded files in that folder.
      </p>
      <pre>
       <code>
        apt-offline get -d /media/[your USB stick]/apt-offline /media/[your USB stick]/cold-apt.sig
       </code>
      </pre>
      <p>
       This fetches the needed files and puts them on the stick. Expect a few fails in bright red. Thats ok. Return the stick to the
       <strong>
        offline
       </strong>
       laptop, and run
      </p>
      <pre>
       <code>
        sudo apt-offline install /media/[your USB stick]/apt-offline
sudo apt-offline set --upgrade /media/[your USB stick]/cold-apt.sig
       </code>
      </pre>
      <p>
       Yup, were doing an upgrade sequence too while were at it. Move the stick to the
       <strong>
        online
       </strong>
       workstation:
      </p>
      <pre>
       <code>
        apt-offline get -d /media/[your USB stick]/apt-offline /media/[your USB stick]/cold-apt.sig
       </code>
      </pre>
      <p>
       and to the
       <strong>
        offline
       </strong>
       :
      </p>
      <pre>
       <code>
        sudo apt-offline install /media/[your USB stick]/apt-offline
       </code>
      </pre>
      <h3>
       Install dependencies and Armory
      </h3>
      <p>
       Armory is an advanced bitcoin wallet that allows for offline storage, so-called cold storage. Its what well be using here. But Armory wont install on a freshly-installed offline Ubuntu 12.04 LTS. Thats a usability problem that is one of the primary reasons for the existence of this walkthrough.
      </p>
      <p>
       Armory depends on three packages that arent present in the base install: python-qt4, python-twisted, and python-psutil. Well use apt-offline to install these, then install Armory. Still on the
       <strong>
        offline
       </strong>
       laptop:
      </p>
      <pre>
       <code>
        sudo apt-offline set --install-packages python-qt4 python-twisted python-psutil
    -- /media/[your USB stick]/cold-apt.sig
       </code>
      </pre>
      <p>
       The two dashes in the command are important, they indicate the end of the package list initiated by install-packages. Also, reflow the command above to
       <em>
        one single line, one single command
       </em>
        its been split into two lines here simply because its too long to display on one.
      </p>
      <p>
       Move the stick to the
       <strong>
        online
       </strong>
       workstation, and run
      </p>
      <pre>
       <code>
        apt-offline get -d /media/[your USB stick]/apt-offline /media/[your USB stick]/cold-apt.sig
       </code>
      </pre>
      <p>
       Also, while were at it, well
       <a href="https://s3.amazonaws.com/bitcoinarmory-releases/armory_0.90-beta_12.04_i386.deb">
        download Armory
       </a>
       in a flavor appropriate for the offline laptop and store the .deb file on the USB stick.
      </p>
      <p>
       Move the stick back to the
       <strong>
        offline
       </strong>
       laptop. Were going to install the dependencies and then Armory.
      </p>
      <pre>
       <code>
        sudo apt-offline install /media/[your USB stick]/apt-offline
sudo apt-get install python-qt4 python-psutil python-twisted
       </code>
      </pre>
      <p>
       And then, lets finally install Armory on the offline machine.
      </p>
      <pre>
       <code>
        sudo dpkg -i /media/[your USB stick]/armory_0.90-beta_12.04_i386.deb
       </code>
      </pre>
      <p>
       If everything went well, you will see
       <strong>
        <em>
         !!! Armory successfully installed !!!
        </em>
       </strong>
       in the terminal window.
      </p>
      <p>
       After this, we dont need the terminal more. Close it.
      </p>
      <h3>
       Create your offline wallet
      </h3>
      <p>
       Start Armory on the offline machine. As you hit the Start button (Windows button on most keyboards) and type Armory, you see three versions of Armory, and well be using the
       <em>
        offline
       </em>
       one. Create your first wallet. Select a password for it (while the computers files are already protected by strong full-disk encryption, defense in depth is always good).
      </p>
      <p>
       Armory will prompt you to back up the wallet.
       <strong>
        Dont back up your wallet at this time.
       </strong>
       The options for backing up the wallet when you create it are severely limited  well be using a much better option.
      </p>
      <p>
       Close all distracting Armory dialogs and messages so that you come back to the main screen, and select your newly-created wallet. Click Wallet properties. In this dialog, well be doing two things: first, well create a
       <em>
        watching-only copy
       </em>
       of the wallet, which we will move to the online workstation. Create a watching-only copy and save the file to the USB stick. Second, well create a secure paper backup of a type we didnt have access to when we had just created the wallet.
      </p>
      <p>
       Click Backup this wallet. Youll be presented with the choice of paper or digital. Choose neither; choose Other backup options at the bottom of the dialog.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the dialog that follows, we have the option of more advanced backup types. Were going to create a
       <em>
        Printable Paper Fragmented Backup
       </em>
       . We wont install a printer to the laptop  its not supposed to be connecting to anything, and were going to be fundamentalist about that. Instead, well be printing to a PDF file we bring to the online workstation and print from there.
      </p>
      <p>
       A fragmented backup means that you print five pieces of paper, and need three of them to restore the backup, any three. Or two out of three. Or nine out of eleven. The numbers are up to you. Id recommend requiring at least three pieces out of any number you choose and placing them in different locations  think disaster recovery; if the offline laptop is in a house fire, youll want to have stored enough backup pieces in different locations to recover your wallet.
      </p>
      <p>
       Check Secure Print. Without this option, the PDF contains your entire wallet
       <em>
        unencrypted,
       </em>
       and since the PDF file will be touching the online workstation and printer while printing it, your unencrypted wallet will be on an online computer without this option  exposing it to hacks. We dont want that. Check Secure Print, then Print all fragments in the leftmost box in the middle frame. (You may have to scroll down to see the button again after checking Secure Print.)
      </p>
      <p>
       Generate a PDF thats to your liking. The offline Armory will present you with a Secure Print code on-screen, to be copied by hand to the individual fragment printouts. Without this code, the backups dont work, and this is why we can safely print the PDF from the online workstation. Copy the SecurePrint code by hand to a separate piece of paper for now.
      </p>
      <p>
       Test the backup as instructed if you like, but were going to do that in a much more thorough fashion shortly anyway.
      </p>
      <p>
       Copy the generated PDF to the USB stick, move the USB stick to the
       <strong>
        online
       </strong>
       workstation, and print the PDF. Keep the note with the Secure Print code beside the printouts for now.
      </p>
      <h3>
       Install Armory on your online workstation
      </h3>
      <p>
       Download the
       <a href="https://bitcoinarmory.com/download/">
        flavor of Armory
       </a>
       thats correct for your online workstation, and install it. When you first run it, youll get the choice of importing/restoring or creating a wallet. Well start by importing our watching-only copy of the wallet that was created on the offline laptop. Thats as easy as choosing Import and selecting the corresponding file on the USB stick.
      </p>
      <p>
       It will install as a Watching-only wallet. Go into wallet properties, edit the owner, and click This is my wallet. That will change its security status to an Offline wallet. The only practical difference is whether its funds are listed as yours in the financial summary.
      </p>
      <p>
       After that, Armory  the online version  will want to install the reference bitcoin client as its back-end and synchronize with the bitcoin network. That will take quite a while, as it loads the entire blockchain. In the meantime, we are going to verify in the harshest possible way that our paper backup works: were going to wipe the entire laptop and reinstall from scratch, simulating that the laptop was destroyed in a house fire and that were restoring the backup to a new offline laptop, and then verifying that the restored wallet works to send coins.
      </p>
      <p>
       Its important in this context that Armory uses so-called
       <em>
        deterministic wallets.
       </em>
       Thats nerdspeak for a wallet backup containing all the addresses that a wallet has generated so far, along with all the addresses the wallet
       <em>
        will ever generate
       </em>
       . Theres no need to re-issue a backup of a particular wallet, ever, just because you have generated more addresses. All the addresses that the wallet will ever contain are embedded in the initial backup.
      </p>
      <p>
       This restore process is a confidence and security exercise. Dont skip it. In case of disaster, you
       <em>
        want
       </em>
       first-hand knowledge that the backup of your many-figures-wallet works, and you want to have used the backup once before so you
       <em>
        know
       </em>
       how to do it.
      </p>
      <h3>
       Wipe and reinstall the offline laptop
      </h3>
      <p>
       Using the USB stick, repeat the Ubuntu Linux install. Wipe the disk completely. Install again with full disk encryption and a strong staple horse correctly to batteries password. Install apt-offline and get the packages, then install Armory again, repeating the above steps.
      </p>
      <p>
       When you start Armory on the second fresh install, it will ask again whether you want to Import/Restore or Create a wallet. This time, well pick Restore, and restore a paper fragmented backup.
      </p>
      <p>
       Youll be presented with a very technical dialog where you have the option of entering data from fragments. Type the backup data from as many fragments as needed, enter the Secure Print code, and click Restore. Youll be asked for a
       <em>
        new password
       </em>
       for the wallet as its restored  here, do note that the paper backup is
       <em>
        unencrypted
       </em>
       . While this can be a comfort in itself if you forget the password, that also means that the problem shifts somewhat from protecting your online computer, to protecting a set of pieces of paper.
      </p>
      <p>
       The wallet should restore fine. Give it a more friendly name than HckfrPlrRT (Restored). With the wallet restored, well try sending some funds to it  and more importantly, from it.
      </p>
      <p>
       At this point, the online workstation is probably still synchronizing with the bitcoin network. You may want to take a break here and go for lunch or so. When you do, dont lose sight and control of your paper backup fragments. They must stay completely under your control until properly distributed  if you leave them on a desk or caf table, youre leaving your wallet unguarded on that table.
      </p>
      <h3>
       Test your offline wallet
      </h3>
      <p>
       When the online Armory has finished synchronizing, send the value of a beer or so to your new wallets first address. It should appear in the online Armory on broadcast, or if Armory wasnt running during the broadcast, as soon as its first confirmation arrives. Wait until six confirmations have passed, so we can send the money back somewhere.
      </p>
      <p>
       When six confirmations have passed, were going to send the coins back (minus the small bitcoin transaction fee). Remember now, these are coins in
       <em>
        cold storage
       </em>
       . Were going to test the cold storage  and not just that, were going to test that it works when restored from backup. The originally created wallet was simulated to be destroyed as we wiped and reinstalled the laptop.
      </p>
      <p>
       To send the coins from cold storage, click Send Bitcoins on the
       <strong>
        online
       </strong>
       workstation and enter a recipient address and amount. Choose Create Unsigned Transaction and confirm the summary. Choose Save as File, and save to the USB stick. Dont close the dialog, but leave it waiting for us to click the Next step button.
      </p>
      <p>
       Move the USB stick to the
       <strong>
        offline
       </strong>
       laptop. Running Armory, select Offline Transactions and Sign Transaction. Load the file with the unsigned transaction, verify its contents as displayed on-screen, and click Sign. Enter the wallet passphrase. The offline Armory will remove the unsigned transaction file on the USB stick and replace it with a signed transaction file.
      </p>
      <p>
       Move the USB stick back to the
       <strong>
        online
       </strong>
       workstation, and click Next step. Click Load file, and load the signed transaction from the USB stick. Transaction details will display. Click Broadcast to execute the signed transaction.
      </p>
      <p>
       You can verify in the blockchain that the transaction has been executed, and you know by your actions that it was signed on the offline computer, which has no connection whatsover to the Internet, nor will ever have one.
      </p>
      <p>
       After this, your setup is complete, and you can move funds into cold storage at a pace youre comfortable with, probably along with further testing of your new cold storage. Just one important step remains.
      </p>
      <h3>
       Distribute the backup fragments
      </h3>
      <p>
       As stated above, your backup contains all the addresses that have been generated
       <em>
        and will ever be generated
       </em>
       by the wallet youve created in Armory, thanks to its deterministic wallets. Theres no need to ever issue a renewed backup of this wallet just because you add more addresses to it.
      </p>
      <p>
       Depending on your security strategy, youll need to choose a way to distribute the paper backup fragments. If theyre intended for a restore by your family in case youre incapacitated, youll want to copy the Secure Print code onto them. If theyre intended only for your own use, you may want to memorize the Secure Print code instead (provided you can trust your memory to recall it in the extreme stress of a disaster recovery situation).
      </p>
      <p>
       Just remember that having observed enough fragments in combination with the Secure Print code is enough to gain access to all your wealth in cold storage without further safeguards or passwords, and devise your backup strategy from there. (Its not necessary for an adversary to be in physical control of a fragment  having observed its data with a camera is enough.)
      </p>
      <div class="clear">
      </div>

       <div id="comment-118040">

        <div class="comment-author vcard">
         <div class="avatar-commentnumber">
          <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-60 photo" height="60" src="//1.gravatar.com/avatar/dd64485cc21c122960e3af826a61eaac?s=60&amp;d=mm&amp;r=r" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/dd64485cc21c122960e3af826a61eaac?s=120&amp;d=mm&amp;r=r 2x" width="60"/>
         </div>
         <cite class="fn">
          Sam Buddy
         </cite>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
         <a href="https://falkvinge.net/2014/02/10/placing-your-crypto-wealth-in-cold-storage-installing-armory-on-ubuntu/#comment-118040">
          February 11, 2014 - 12:06
         </a>
        </div>
        <p>
         A few minor remarks:
        </p>
        <p>
          Perhaps the wifi interface is soldered to the motherboard in some laptops, but at least sometimes its a separate card that can be removed. That is obviously the surest way to disable it. Youll probably need to remove some screws to open the cover, but not disassemble the whole laptop.
        </p>
        <p>
          You might want to disable Bluetooth too. These days it may be included on the wifi card. In an old laptop its probably a separate card. If theres a 3G or LTE card, remove that too.
        </p>
        <p>
          I suggest doing things in a slightly different order: After making the paper backup, first transfer a small amount to the offline wallet, *then* wipe and restore, and then send the money back. That way youll see that funds deposited after the backup was made are still accessible after restoring.
        </p>
        <div class="reply">
         <a aria-label="Reply to Sam Buddy" class="comment-reply-link" href="/2014/02/10/placing-your-crypto-wealth-in-cold-storage-installing-armory-on-ubuntu/?replytocom=118040#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-118040", "118040", "respond", "17762" )'>

          <p>
           Good points about wi-fi hardware. In my specific case, the wi-fi circuits were already hosed, so I didnt have to go all medieval on this particular laptop.
          </p>
          <p>
           In theory, it could even be enough to uninstall and remove the wi-fi drivers, as they would have no way of reappearing on their own. Thats slightly weaker than killing the hardware, though.
          </p>
          <p>
           Im slightly confused as to your third point, the reshuffling. Youll see the funds only on the online machine, through the watch-only wallet  the offline machine will never display any funds, as it has no connection to the blockchain or any servers. The online machine is oblivious to when or if the offline machine is being reinstalled, that doesnt affect whats displayed on it, and the offline machine never displays any funds in the first place. Whats the gain by this reshuffle?
          </p>
          <p>
           Cheers,
           <br/>
           Rick
          </p>

        <div class="comment-author vcard">
         <div class="avatar-commentnumber">
          <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-60 photo" height="60" src="//0.gravatar.com/avatar/3a4651447c3ffc65b7a04611a4355a33?s=60&amp;d=mm&amp;r=r" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/3a4651447c3ffc65b7a04611a4355a33?s=120&amp;d=mm&amp;r=r 2x" width="60"/>
         </div>
         <cite class="fn">
          A
         </cite>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
         <a href="https://falkvinge.net/2014/02/10/placing-your-crypto-wealth-in-cold-storage-installing-armory-on-ubuntu/#comment-118061">
          February 11, 2014 - 22:20
         </a>
        </div>
        <p>
         dpkg -i is insecure, and, at least for apt-offline, unnecessary. Youre bypassing signature checking leaving you vulnerable to man-in-the-middle attacks, which can compromise your systems security. Please replace `dpkg -i /apt-offline` with `apt-get install apt-offline` and add a warning for `dpkg -i /armory`.
        </p>
        <p>
         Also, please note you wont get security updates for armory that way.
        </p>
        <div class="reply">
         <a aria-label="Reply to A" class="comment-reply-link" href="/2014/02/10/placing-your-crypto-wealth-in-cold-storage-installing-armory-on-ubuntu/?replytocom=118061#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-118061", "118061", "respond", "17762" )'>

          <div class="commentnumber">
           6.1
          </div>
          <div class="comment-author vcard">
           <div class="avatar-commentnumber">
            <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-60 photo" height="60" src="//0.gravatar.com/avatar/fb99b6fc26233b62407a0f60b3ddcce3?s=60&amp;d=mm&amp;r=r" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/fb99b6fc26233b62407a0f60b3ddcce3?s=120&amp;d=mm&amp;r=r 2x" width="60"/>
           </div>
           <cite class="fn">
            <a class="url" href="http://falkvinge.net/" rel="external nofollow">
             Rick Falkvinge
            </a>
           </cite>
          </div>
          <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
           <a href="https://falkvinge.net/2014/02/10/placing-your-crypto-wealth-in-cold-storage-installing-armory-on-ubuntu/#comment-118065">
            February 12, 2014 - 01:34
           </a>
          </div>
          <p>
           An old desktop is definitely more preferable just because it typically doesnt contain wi-fi, as you point out, and Im glad to hear you have old ones and the ability to put them to good use.
          </p>
          <p>
           Forgive me if Im being insensitive, but Im just curious as we seem to have different frames of reference here, so let me explain where I come from. I tend to keep old laptops that are defective in one way or another because they dont take a lot of space, they kind of get stuck on a shelf if I should find use and need for them, but old desktop machines that are similarly defective are very bulky and storage space comes at a premium, so I tend to get rid of them in one way or another.
          </p>
          <p>
           This particular laptop of mine had broken networking and had a bad screen on top of that, which made it useless as a production laptop for anything else but this exact purpose. I didnt need to modify it; it was already permanently offline.
          </p>
          <p>
           I was assuming most people reading this article would be in a similar situation. Apparently, your situation is almost the opposite  you write that you have a surplus of old desktop machines. For this exercise, thats far preferable as theyre much easier to keep offline and also dont require a lot of memory or processing power; I just wasnt aware of the situation.
          </p>
          <p>
           Cheers,
           <br/>
           Rick
          </p>

      <li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-118399">
       <div id="comment-118399">
        <div class="commentnumber">
         10
        </div>
        <div class="comment-author vcard">
         <div class="avatar-commentnumber">
          <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-60 photo" height="60" src="//1.gravatar.com/avatar/13f3ada6f3002c11d93871fcd2178c66?s=60&amp;d=mm&amp;r=r" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/13f3ada6f3002c11d93871fcd2178c66?s=120&amp;d=mm&amp;r=r 2x" width="60"/>
         </div>
         <cite class="fn">
          <a class="url" href="http://bitcoinarmory.com" rel="external nofollow">
           etotheipi
          </a>
         </cite>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
         <a href="https://falkvinge.net/2014/02/10/placing-your-crypto-wealth-in-cold-storage-installing-armory-on-ubuntu/#comment-118399">
          March 3, 2014 - 02:11
         </a>
        </div>
        <p>
         The bitcoinarmory.com website has Offline Bundles on their download page, specifically to avoid the complexities in this posting. Not to say the whole thing isnt complex, but the offline bundle was designed to be double-click-install on the first boot of the fresh OS.
        </p>
        <p>
         (1) Download Ubuntu 12.04-32bit
         <br/>
         (2) Install 12.04-32bit on offline system
         <br/>
         (3) Download Armory offline bundle for 12.04-32bit
         <br/>
         (3a) Download and verify signatures (instructions on download page)
         <br/>
         (4) Unpack offline bundle on offline computer, double-click InstallRunInTerminal.sh
         <br/>
         (5) Click Run In Terminal when asked, type password
         <br/>
         (6) Run Armory
        </p>
        <div class="reply">
         <a aria-label="Reply to etotheipi" class="comment-reply-link" href="/2014/02/10/placing-your-crypto-wealth-in-cold-storage-installing-armory-on-ubuntu/?replytocom=118399#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-118399", "118399", "respond", "17762" )'>

        <p>
         I got a good signature file in the first instance (sudo apt-offline set update /media/[your USB stick]/cold-apt.sig) and was able to download on the online PC (Windows 7) and then install the updates on the offline laptop (Ubuntu), but when I got to the following two lines, I have been getting empty signature files:
        </p>
        <p>
         sudo apt-offline set upgrade /media/[your USB stick]/cold-apt.sig
        </p>
        <p>
         sudo apt-offline set install-packages python-qt4 python-twisted python-psutil
         <br/>
          /media/[your USB stick]/cold-apt.sig
        </p>

         In addition, I get the following message regarding python-twisted and python-psutil:
        </p>

         <br/>
         Unable to locate package python-psutil
        </p>
